<p><i><b>Megami Tensei</b></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">女神転生</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, often translated as "Rebirth of the Goddess")</span>, commonly abbreviated as <i><b>MegaTen</b></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">メガテン</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> and marketed overseas as <i><b>Shin Megami Tensei</b></i> (originally <i>Revelations</i>), is a Japanese <a href="/wiki/Media_franchise" title="Media franchise">media franchise</a> created by Kouji Okada (credited as Cozy Okada in English), Ginichiro Suzuki, and Kazunari Suzuki. Primarily developed by <a href="/wiki/Atlus" title="Atlus">Atlus</a> and currently owned by <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a>, the franchise consists of multiple subseries and covers multiple <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_video_game" title="Role-playing video game">role-playing</a> genres including <a href="/wiki/Tactical_role-playing_game" title="Tactical role-playing game">tactical role-playing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Action_role-playing_game" title="Action role-playing game">action role-playing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_role-playing_game" title="Massively multiplayer online role-playing game">massively multiplayer online role-playing</a>. The first two titles in the series were published by <a href="/wiki/Bandai_Namco_Entertainment" title="Bandai Namco Entertainment">Bandai Namco</a> (then Namco), but have been almost always published by Atlus in Japan and North America since the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei" title="Shin Megami Tensei">Shin Megami Tensei</a></i>. For Europe, Atlus publishes the games through third-party companies.</p>
<p>The series was originally based on <i>Digital Devil Story</i>, a science fiction novel series by Aya Nishitani. The series takes its name from the first book's subtitle. Most <i>Megami Tensei</i> titles are stand-alone entries with their own stories and characters. Recurring elements include plot themes, a story shaped by player choices, and <a href="/wiki/Game_mechanics" title="Game mechanics">game mechanics</a>, the most notable being the ability to fight using and often recruit creatures (Demons, Persona) to aid the player in battle. Elements of multiple philosophies and religions, occultism, cyberpunk and early science fiction have all been incorporated into the series at different times.</p>
<p>While not maintaining as high a profile as series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Final_Fantasy" title="Final Fantasy">Final Fantasy</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dragon_Quest" title="Dragon Quest">Dragon Quest</a></i>, it is highly popular in Japan and maintains a strong following in the west, finding critical and commercial success. The series has become well known for its artistic direction, challenging gameplay, and music. The series has equally raised controversy over its mature content, dark themes, and use of Christian religious imagery. Additional media includes manga adaptations, and anime films and TV series.</p>
<p>In Japan, some games in the series do not use the exact "Megami Tensei" name itself, such as the <i>Persona</i> subseries. However, all localizations have used the "Shin Megami Tensei" moniker since the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Nocturne" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Nocturne">Shin Megami Tensei: Nocturne</a></i> in 2003. Most of the early titles have not been localized; many of these were on Nintendo platforms, and for years Nintendo of America generally treated religious references as taboo.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles">Titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Games">Games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Megami_Tensei_video_games" title="List of Megami Tensei video games">List of Megami Tensei video games</a></div>
<p>The first installment in the franchise, <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Devil_Story:_Megami_Tensei" title="Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei">Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei</a></i>, was released on September 11, 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DDS1release_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-DDS1release-1">[1]</a></sup> The following entries have nearly always been unrelated to each other except in carrying over thematic and gameplay elements. The <i>Megami Tensei</i> games, and the later <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> titles form the core of the series, while other subseries such as <i>Persona</i> and <i>Devil Summoner</i> are spin-offs marketed as part of the franchise. There are also stand-alone spin-off titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guide1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup></p>

<table class="infobox" style="float: right">
<tr>
<td>
<p align="center"><i><b>Megami Tensei/Shin Megami Tensei</b></i></p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Devil_Story:_Megami_Tensei" title="Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei">Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei</a></i></li>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_II" title="Shin Megami Tensei II">Shin Megami Tensei II</a></i></li>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Nine" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Nine">Shin Megami Tensei: Nine</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Nocturne" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Nocturne">Shin Megami Tensei III: Nocturne</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Imagine" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Imagine">Shin Megami Tensei: Imagine</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Strange_Journey" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Strange Journey">Shin Megami Tensei: Strange Journey</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_IV" title="Shin Megami Tensei IV">Shin Megami Tensei IV</a></i>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_IV:_Apocalypse" title="Shin Megami Tensei IV: Apocalypse">Shin Megami Tensei IV: Apocalypse</a></i></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Two entries have been released for the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Nintendo Entertainment System">Famicom</a>: <i>Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei</i> in 1987, and <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Devil_Story:_Megami_Tensei_II" title="Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei II">Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei II</a></i> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DDS1release_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-DDS1release-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DDS2release_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-DDS2release-4">[4]</a></sup> The two titles are unrelated to each other in terms of story, and each introduced the basic gameplay and story mechanics that would come to define the series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> Two entries were released for the <a href="/wiki/Super_Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Super Nintendo Entertainment System">Super Famicom</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei" title="Shin Megami Tensei">Shin Megami Tensei</a></i> in 1992, and <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_II" title="Shin Megami Tensei II">Shin Megami Tensei II</a></i> in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a nine-year gap, <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Nocturne" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Nocturne">Shin Megami Tensei III: Nocturne</a></i> was released in 2003 for the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_2" title="PlayStation 2">PlayStation 2</a>. Its <i>Maniax Edition</i> director's cut was released in Japan and North America in 2004, and in Europe in 2005. The numeral was dropped for its North American release, and its title changed to <i>Shin Megami Tensei: Lucifer's Call</i> in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NocturneJapan_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NocturneJapan-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NocturneManiax_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NocturneManiax-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NocturneNA_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NocturneNA-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NocturneEuro_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NocturneEuro-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GamaList_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-GamaList-10">[10]</a></sup> The next entry, <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Strange_Journey" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Strange Journey">Shin Megami Tensei: Strange Journey</a></i>, was released for the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_DS" title="Nintendo DS">Nintendo DS</a> in 2009 in Japan and 2010 in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTSTrelease_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTSTrelease-11">[11]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_IV" title="Shin Megami Tensei IV">Shin Megami Tensei IV</a></i> for the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_3DS" title="Nintendo 3DS">Nintendo 3DS</a> was released in 2013 in Japan and North America, and a year later in Europe as a digital-only release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMT4JPrelease_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMT4JPrelease-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMT4NArelease_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMT4NArelease-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMT4EUrelease_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMT4EUrelease-14">[14]</a></sup> Another game set in the same universe, <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_IV:_Apocalypse" title="Shin Megami Tensei IV: Apocalypse">Shin Megami Tensei IV: Apocalypse</a></i>, was released for the 3DS in February 2016 in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamSMTIVF_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FamSMTIVF-15">[15]</a></sup> Two titles are in development: one for the 3DS, and one for the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Switch" title="Nintendo Switch">Nintendo Switch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the main series, three <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> spin-off games exist. The first is <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_If..." title="Shin Megami Tensei If...">Shin Megami Tensei If...</a></i>, released in the same year and on the same system as <i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTif_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTif-17">[17]</a></sup> The second, <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Nine" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Nine">Shin Megami Tensei: Nine</a></i>, was released for the <a href="/wiki/Xbox_(console)" title="Xbox (console)">Xbox</a> in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTnine_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTnine-18">[18]</a></sup> Originally designed as a <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_role-playing_game" title="Massively multiplayer online role-playing game">massively multiplayer online role-playing game</a> (MMORPG), it was later split into a dual single-player and multiplayer package, and the single-player version released first. The online version was delayed and eventually cancelled as the developers could not manage the required online capacities using <a href="/wiki/Xbox_Live" title="Xbox Live">Xbox Live</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGnine_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPGnine-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NINEcancelled_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NINEcancelled-20">[20]</a></sup> A true MMORPG, <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Imagine" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Imagine">Shin Megami Tensei: Imagine</a></i>, was released for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> in 2007 in Japan, 2008 in North America, and 2009 in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTIjapan_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTIjapan-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTIwest1_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTIwest1-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTIwest2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTIwest2-23">[23]</a></sup> Western service was terminated in 2014 when <a href="/wiki/Marvelous_USA" title="Marvelous USA">Marvelous USA</a>, the game's then-handlers, shut down their PC Online game department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ImagineEnd_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ImagineEnd-24">[24]</a></sup> Its Japanese service ended in May 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTIjpClosure_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTIjpClosure-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Persona"><i>Persona</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Persona">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Persona_(series)" title="Persona (series)">Persona (series)</a></div>
<p><i>Persona</i> is the largest and most popular spin-off from the <i>Megami Tensei</i> series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersona_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersona-26">[26]</a></sup> The first entry in the series, <i>Megami Ibunroku Persona</i> (originally released overseas as <i><a href="/wiki/Revelations:_Persona" title="Revelations: Persona">Revelations: Persona</a></i>) was released in 1996 in Japan and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechRevelations_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-TechRevelations-28">[28]</a></sup> The first <i>Persona 2</i> title, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_2:_Innocent_Sin" title="Persona 2: Innocent Sin">Innocent Sin</a></i>, was released in 1999 in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup> The second game, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_2:_Eternal_Punishment" title="Persona 2: Eternal Punishment">Eternal Punishment</a></i>, was released in 2000 in Japan and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGNpunishment_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGNpunishment-29">[29]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Persona_3" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Persona 3">Persona 3</a></i> was released in 2006 in Japan, 2007 in North America, and 2008 in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3jp_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3jp-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3na_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3na-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3eu_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3eu-32">[32]</a></sup> Its sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Persona_4" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Persona 4">Persona 4</a></i>, was released in 2008 in Japan and North America, and in 2009 in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4jp_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4jp-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4na_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4na-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4eu_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4eu-35">[35]</a></sup> A sixth entry in the series, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_5" title="Persona 5">Persona 5</a></i>, was released in Japan on September 15, 2016, and will be released in North America and Europe on April 4, 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P5release_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-P5release-36">[36]</a></sup> In addition to the main <i>Persona</i> games are spin-offs, so far focused on <i>Persona 3</i> and <i>4</i>: the canon spin-off <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_Q:_Shadow_of_the_Labyrinth" title="Persona Q: Shadow of the Labyrinth">Persona Q: Shadow of the Labyrinth</a></i>, two fighting games <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_4_Arena" title="Persona 4 Arena">Persona 4 Arena</a></i> and its sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_4_Arena_Ultimax" title="Persona 4 Arena Ultimax">Arena Ultimax</a></i>, and rhythm game <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_4:_Dancing_All_Night" title="Persona 4: Dancing All Night">Persona 4: Dancing All Night</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4arena_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4arena-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PolyPersona_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-PolyPersona-38">[38]</a></sup> While <i>Persona 3</i> and <i>4</i> used the <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> moniker, it was dropped for the <i>Persona 4 Arena</i> duology and <i>Persona 4 Golden</i> as it would have made the titles too long to be practical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersona_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersona-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamitsuTitles_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FamitsuTitles-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_spin-offs">Other spin-offs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other spin-offs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Aside from <i>Persona</i>, there are other spin-off series covering multiple genres. After the release of <i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i>, Atlus began focusing work on building spin-offs and subseries that would form part of the <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guide1-2">[2]</a></sup> The <i>Devil Summoner</i> subseries began in 1995 with the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Summoner" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Summoner">Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Summoner</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> It was followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Summoner:_Soul_Hackers" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Summoner: Soul Hackers">Devil Summoner: Soul Hackers</a></i> in 1997,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup> and two prequels set in 1920s Tokyo and revolving around demon summoner Raidou Kuzunoha: <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Summoner:_Raidou_Kuzunoha_vs._The_Soulless_Army" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Summoner: Raidou Kuzunoha vs. The Soulless Army">Raidou Kuzunoha vs. The Soulless Army</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Summoner_2:_Raidou_Kuzunoha_vs._King_Abaddon" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Summoner 2: Raidou Kuzunoha vs. King Abaddon">Raidou Kuzunoha vs. King Abaddon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NocturneJapan_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-NocturneJapan-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after <i>Nocturne</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s release, a duology titled <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Digital_Devil_Saga" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Digital Devil Saga">Digital Devil Saga</a></i> (<i>Digital Devil Saga: Avatar Tuner</i> in Japan) was created based around similar systems to <i>Nocturne</i>, and was also intended as a more accessible gaming experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGdds_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPGdds-40">[40]</a></sup> Two tactical role-playing games have been developed by Atlus for the DS under the <i>Devil Survivor</i> moniker: the original <a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Survivor" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Survivor"><i>Devil Survivor</i></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Survivor_2" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Survivor 2">Devil Survivor 2</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSfirst_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSfirst-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSsecond_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSsecond-42">[42]</a></sup> Both have received expanded ports for the 3DS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3DSfirst_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-3DSfirst-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3DSsecond_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-3DSsecond-44">[44]</a></sup> Other subseries include <i><a href="/wiki/Megami_Tensei_Gaiden:_Last_Bible" title="Megami Tensei Gaiden: Last Bible">Last Bible</a></i>, a series aimed at a younger audience and using a pure fantasy setting;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei:_Devil_Children" title="Shin Megami Tensei: Devil Children">Devil Children</a></i>, which was inspired by the popular <i>Pokemon</i> series;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hcg101pg1_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-hcg101pg1-45">[45]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Majin_Tensei" title="Majin Tensei">Majin Tensei</a></i>, a series strategy games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hcg101Majin_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-hcg101Majin-46">[46]</a></sup> Two notable stand-alone spin-offs are <a href="/wiki/Action_game" title="Action game">action</a> spin-off <i><a href="/wiki/Jack_Bros." title="Jack Bros.">Jack Bros.</a></i>; and <i><a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Mirage_Sessions_%E2%99%AFFE" title="Tokyo Mirage Sessions ♯FE">Tokyo Mirage Sessions ♯FE</a></i>, a crossover with <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_Systems" title="Intelligent Systems">Intelligent Systems</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Fire_Emblem" title="Fire Emblem">Fire Emblem</a></i> series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGhistory_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPGhistory-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTfireemblem_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTfireemblem-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_media">Related media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Related media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Megami_Tensei_media" title="List of Megami Tensei media">List of Megami Tensei media</a></div>
<p>Several titles in the franchise have received <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> adaptations: <i>Persona 3</i> received both a four-part theatrical adaptation (<i><a href="/wiki/Persona_3_The_Movie:_No._1,_Spring_of_Birth" title="Persona 3 The Movie: No. 1, Spring of Birth">No. 1, Spring of Birth</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_3_The_Movie:_No._2,_Midsummer_Knight%27s_Dream" title="Persona 3 The Movie: No. 2, Midsummer Knight's Dream">No. 2, Midsummer Knight's Dream</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_3_The_Movie:_No._3,_Falling_Down" title="Persona 3 The Movie: No. 3, Falling Down">No. 3, Falling Down</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_3_The_Movie:_No._4,_Winter_of_Rebirth" title="Persona 3 The Movie: No. 4, Winter of Rebirth">No. 4, Winter of Rebirth</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3movie1_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3movie1-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3movie2_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3movie2-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3movie3_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3movie3-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3movie4_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3movie4-52">[52]</a></sup> and a spin-off series titled <i><a href="/wiki/Persona:_Trinity_Soul" title="Persona: Trinity Soul">Persona: Trinity Soul</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3trinity_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3trinity-53">[53]</a></sup> <i>Persona 4</i> received two adaptations: <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_4:_The_Animation" title="Persona 4: The Animation">Persona 4: The Animation</a></i>, based on the original game, and <i>Persona 4: The Golden Animation</i>, based on its expanded <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Vita" title="PlayStation Vita">PlayStation Vita</a> port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4anime1_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4anime1-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4anime2_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4anime2-55">[55]</a></sup> A live-action television series based on the original <i>Devil Summoner</i> was broadcast between 1997 and 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DevilTV_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-DevilTV-56">[56]</a></sup> <i>Devil Survivor 2</i> also received an anime adaptation <a href="/wiki/Devil_Survivor_2:_The_Animation" title="Devil Survivor 2: The Animation">of the same name</a>, while the <i>Devil Children</i> series received two anime adaptations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSanime_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSanime-57">[57]</a></sup> Multiple <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> and <i>Persona</i> titles have received manga and CD drama adaptations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3manga_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3manga-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4manga_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4manga-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PQmanga_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-PQmanga-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSmanga_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSmanga-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NocturneCD_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-NocturneCD-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSdrama_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSdrama-63">[63]</a></sup> Action figures and merchandise related to <i>Persona</i> have also been produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P3figures_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-P3figures-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4figures_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4figures-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_elements">Common elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Common elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Despite most games in the series taking place in different continuities, they do share certain elements. One of its defining traits is it being set in a contemporary urban environment, specifically modern-day Tokyo. Post-apocalyptic elements are a recurring feature in settings and narratives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup> This choice was originally made to set the game apart from other fantasy-based gaming franchises of the time, as modern day Tokyo was rarely seen in games as opposed to versions of it from the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4Ginfo_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-4Ginfo-67">[67]</a></sup> The <i>Persona</i> series takes place exclusively within this setting, spanning a single continuity and mostly focusing on the exploits of a group of young people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersona_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersona-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upPersona4_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-1upPersona4-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i> is one of the notable early exceptions to the series' common setting, as it is set in a science fiction-styled future despite still including fantasy elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaneko3_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaneko3-69">[69]</a></sup> The <i>Last Bible</i> series also shifted to a full fantasy setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup> Two more recent notable departures were <i>Strange Journey</i>, which shifted the focus to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> to portray the threat on a global scale, and <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV</i>, which included a medieval-stage society existing separately from a modern-day Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EshidaInterview_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-EshidaInterview-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polygon_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon-71">[71]</a></sup> The <i>Devil Summoner</i> games take the form of modern-day detective stories as opposed to post-apocalyptic settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TechSMT_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-TechSMT-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The series title translates as "Rebirth of the Goddess": this has carried over into the current <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> series, which has been officially translated as "True Goddess Metempsychosis". The word "<a href="/wiki/Metempsychosis" title="Metempsychosis">Metempsychosis</a>" refers to the cycle of reincarnation that ties into many <i>Megami Tensei</i> stories. The reborn goddess of the title has multiple meanings: it refers to a female character in each game that could be interpreted as the goddess, and is also representative of the drastic changes a location undergoes during a game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guide1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> The concept of reincarnation was also included in narratives and gameplay mechanics to tie in with these themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> The series' overarching title has been truncated to "<i>MegaTen</i>" by series fans. Originating in Japan, the abbreviation has become a common term for the series among its fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GFsummoner_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-GFsummoner-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MegaEngl_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-MegaEngl-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gameplay">Gameplay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Gameplay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="350" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e1/SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg/190px-SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e1/SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg/285px-SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e1/SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SMT_IV_gameplay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Screenshot of the Press Turn system as it appears in <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV</i>. Allied demons include designs by <a href="/wiki/Kazuma_Kaneko" title="Kazuma Kaneko">Kazuma Kaneko</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The gameplay in the series has become notable for its high difficulty, along with several mechanics that have endured through the years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscapeInterview_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-EscapeInterview-74">[74]</a></sup> A key element present since the first <i>Megami Tensei</i> is the ability to recruit demons to fight alongside the player in battle, alongside the ability to fuse two different demons together to create a more powerful demon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> Equivalents to these systems appear in the later <i>Persona</i> titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1UPpersona_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-1UPpersona-75">[75]</a></sup> The game's most recognizable battle system is the Press Turn system, first introduced in <i>Nocturne</i>. The Press Turn System is a turn-based battle mechanic governing both the player party and enemies, where either party are rewarded an extra turn for striking an enemy's weakness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGdds_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPGdds-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTmoon_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTmoon-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InterviewDS_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-InterviewDS-77">[77]</a></sup> A Moon Phase System or equivalent, in which phases of the moon or changes in the weather affected the behavior of enemies, is also featured in multiple games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTmoon_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-SMTmoon-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silicon1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silicon1-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The layout of the first two <i>Megami Tensei</i> games were noticeably different from later games: <i>Megami Tensei</i> used a 3D first-person perspective, while <i>Megami Tensei II</i> used a combination of first-person 3D displays for battle and top-down 2D displays for navigation. The change was suggested by staff members who did not want players getting lost in a large 3D environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> The 2D/first person viewpoint continued until <i>Nocturne</i>, which switched to a third-person perspective. This was done due to a condition similar to car sickness called "3D sickness" with first person shooters in Japan at the time: the developers wanted something for players to focus on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide1_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Guide1-2">[2]</a></sup> The first-person perspective was reintroduced in <i>Strange Journey</i>, and incorporated into <i>IV</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s battles along with navigable 3D environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJgameplay_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-SJgameplay-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSpot_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSpot-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plots_and_themes">Plots and themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Plots and themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Each title focuses on the extraordinary invading the ordinary world, though the two main <i>Megami Tensei</i> series focus on different things: <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> focuses more on the main protagonist gaining the power needed to survive in a world ruled over by tyrannical deities, while <i>Persona</i> focuses on interpersonal relationships and the psychology of a group of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EshidaInterview_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-EshidaInterview-70">[70]</a></sup> That protagonist is generally male within the <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> titles: while a female lead or the ability to choose a lead's gender is not out of the question, many staff feel that <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> lead roles are better suited to a male character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silicon1_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Silicon1-78">[78]</a></sup> Throughout its lifetime, the series has incorporated elements of <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a>, various world mythologies and religions including <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, early science fiction, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jungian_psychology" title="Jungian psychology">Jungian psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archetypal_pedagogy" title="Archetypal pedagogy">archetypes</a>, occultism, <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk</a>, and cyberpunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide1_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Guide1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polygon_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscapeInterview_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-EscapeInterview-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide3_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guide3-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upinter_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-1upinter-82">[82]</a></sup> The science fiction and fantasy elements are brought together and unified through the use of philosophical concepts, enabling a blending of concepts and aesthetics that might normally clash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscapeInterview_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-EscapeInterview-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The stories of the core <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> titles frequently include fighting against a tyrannical God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide2_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guide2-83">[83]</a></sup> The method of story-telling in the series can involve traditional use of cutscenes and spoken dialogue (<i>Persona</i>, <i>Digital Devil Saga</i>), or a text-based minimalist approach that places emphasis on atmosphere (<i>Nocturne</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscapeInterview_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-EscapeInterview-74">[74]</a></sup> A tradition within the core <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> series is to focus on a single playable character as opposed to a group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upinter_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-1upinter-82">[82]</a></sup> A recurring character is Lucifer, the fallen angel who stands against God and is portrayed in multiple forms to represent his omnipotence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KanekoDesign_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-KanekoDesign-84">[84]</a></sup> Since <i>Megami Tensei II</i>, the series has used a morality-based decision system, where the player's actions affect the outcome of the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> In <i>Megami Tensei II</i>, the alignments were defined as "Law" (the forces of God) and "Chaos" (the army of Lucifer).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup> In future games, an additional "Neutral" route was included where the player could reject both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EshidaInterview_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-EshidaInterview-70">[70]</a></sup> Selected games have been thematically or otherwise linked to a particular alignment. <i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i>, due to events prior to the story, focuses on the "Law" alignment. For <i>Nocturne</i>, all the characters were roughly aligned with "Chaos", which was done both to bring variety to the series and allow the development team more creative freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide4_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guide4-86">[86]</a></sup> <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV: Apocalypse</i> is restricted to a "Neutral" alignment while still having multiple endings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilNico_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-SilNico-87">[87]</a></sup> The three-tired alignment was used in <i>Strange Journey</i>, and continued into <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EshidaInterview_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-EshidaInterview-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silicon2_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silicon2-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_history">Development and history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Development and history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Megami Tensei</i> series began life as a media expansion of the <i>Digital Devil Story</i> series, a set of science-fantasy novels written by Aya Nishitani during the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup> The series' creators were Kouji Okada (credited as Cozy Okada in English), Ginichiro Suzuki, and Ginichiro's son Kazunari.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KoujiSMT_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-KoujiSMT-89">[89]</a></sup> The first book in the <i>Digital Devil Story</i> series, <i>Megami Tensei</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">女神転生</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, lit. "Reincarnation of the Goddess")</span>, provided the title for the original game, while the game's story was based on both the first book and the third book <i>Tensei no Shuuen</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja">転生の終焉</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>, lit. "Demise of the Reincarnation")</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamMobile_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FamMobile-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANNdigital_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANNdigital-91">[91]</a></sup> The game was developed at <a href="/wiki/Atlus" title="Atlus">Atlus</a> and published by <a href="/wiki/Bandai_Namco_Entertainment" title="Bandai Namco Entertainment">Bandai Namco</a> (then Namco). Although they wanted to incorporate as much of the original story as possible, the limited capabilities of the Famicom made this goal nearly impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-5"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANNdigital_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-ANNdigital-91">[91]</a></sup> The game proved popular in Japan, and effectively launched the <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise, with its more ambitious direct sequel following in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANNdigital_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-ANNdigital-91">[91]</a></sup> During the development of <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i>, which was driven by the concept of a Super Famicom game with the company's brand on it, the team slowly decided that they wanted to break the then-current gaming status quo using its aesthetic and content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaneko1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaneko1-92">[92]</a></sup> Despite this attitude, the staff considered <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> to be a remake of <i>Megami Tensei II</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-12"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> In many of these earlier games, staff members at Atlus had cameos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-13"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The majority of the <i>Megami Tensei</i> series is developed by Atlus' R&amp;D Department 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTdeveloper_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTdeveloper-93">[93]</a></sup> Other developers have been involved with the series: these include Multimedia Intelligence Transfer (<i>Last Bible</i> series), Lancarse (<i>Strange Journey</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAVE_(company)" title="CAVE (company)">CAVE</a> (<i>Imagine</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Nex_Entertainment" title="Nex Entertainment">Nex Entertainment</a> (<i>Nine</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Arc_System_Works" title="Arc System Works">Arc System Works</a> (<i>Persona 4 Arena</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTIwest1_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-SMTIwest1-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EshidaInterview_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-EshidaInterview-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTNnex_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTNnex-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BibleDeveloper1_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-BibleDeveloper1-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BibleDeveloper2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-BibleDeveloper2-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGNinterview_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGNinterview-97">[97]</a></sup> Most of the games up to 2003 were handled by Okada, but when he departed to form his own company <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(game_company)" title="Gaia (game company)">Gaia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazuma_Kaneko" title="Kazuma Kaneko">Kazuma Kaneko</a> became the series' creative director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KanekoDesign_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-KanekoDesign-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KoujiSMT_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-KoujiSMT-89">[89]</a></sup> There are two main writers in the franchise: Shogo Isogai and Ryutaro Ito.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-14"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CubedSMT_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-CubedSMT-98">[98]</a></sup> Ito first worked on <i>Megami Tensei II</i>, joining the team after development to write the script, along with working with the script and being part of the debug team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-15"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> Isogai's first work for the series was the script for <i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CubedSMT_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-CubedSMT-98">[98]</a></sup> The next entry <i>If...</i> was also written by Ito, and designed as a departure from the grand scale of previous games, instead being set within a cloistered school environment. His final work for the series was the first <i>Devil Summoner</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-16"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup> Isogai also worked on <i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i> and <i>If...</i>, and later worked on multiple <i>Devil Summoner</i> games, <i>Nocturne</i> and <i>Strange Journey</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CubedSMT_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-CubedSMT-98">[98]</a></sup> The music for the first five main <i>Megami Tensei</i> titles was composed by Tsukasa Masuko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItoInterview_5-17"><a href="#cite_note-ItoInterview-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGmusic_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPGmusic-99">[99]</a></sup> For <i>Nocturne</i>, <a href="/wiki/Shoji_Meguro" title="Shoji Meguro">Shoji Meguro</a>, who had done work on earlier spin-off titles, was brought in. He later became well known for his work on the <i>Persona</i> titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upMusic_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-1upMusic-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShojiInterview_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShojiInterview-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PersonaBlog_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-PersonaBlog-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_design">Art design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Art design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persona_3_AD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="300" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1e/Persona_3_AD.jpg/220px-Persona_3_AD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1e/Persona_3_AD.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persona_3_AD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Japanese ad for <i>Persona 3</i>, created by the game's art director, <a href="/wiki/Shigenori_Soejima" title="Shigenori Soejima">Shigenori Soejima</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> and <i>Persona</i> art styles have been defined by two different artists: Kazuma Kaneko and <a href="/wiki/Shigenori_Soejima" title="Shigenori Soejima">Shigenori Soejima</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup> Kaneko had a long history with the series, having done some work on the original <i>Megami Tensei</i> titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup> His first prominent work for the series was on <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i>, who worked on both the sprite art and promotional artwork for the game's characters and demons. He was also responsible for suggesting many of the game's darker features, defining the series' eventual identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaneko1_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaneko1-92">[92]</a></sup> Before designing each demon, Kaneko looks up his chosen subject to get their mythological background, and uses that in their design. Many of Kaneko's demon designs were influenced by both creatures and deities from world mythology, and monsters from popular culture like <a href="/wiki/Godzilla" title="Godzilla">Godzilla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscapeInterview_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-EscapeInterview-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup> Alongside working on <i>Shin Megami Tensei II</i>, <i>If...</i> and <i>Nocturne</i>, he also did character designs for the first three <i>Persona</i> games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaneko3_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaneko3-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-4"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaneko1_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kaneko1-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaneko2_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaneko2-103">[103]</a></sup> Kaneko's style has been described as "cold [and] stoic", evolving into that state over time to keep the artwork as close as possible to the in-game render.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-5"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Soejima's first work for the series was as part of the digital coloring team for the first <i>Devil Summoner</i>. He later had minor roles in artwork and character design in the first <i>Persona</i> and <i>Soul Hackers</i>. He later did the secondary characters for the <i>Persona 2</i> duology, and was also part of the team checking over the PlayStation ports of the first three <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> games, as well as minor work on <i>Nocturne</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup> Soejima was chosen as the lead designer for <i>Persona 3</i> by Kaneko, as Kaneko wanted the younger staff members to gain experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-6"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup> <i>Persona 3</i> proved challenging for Soejima as he needed to refine his drawing style and take the expectations of series fans into account. He would go on to design for <i>Persona 3 FES</i> and <i>Portable</i>, <i>Persona 4</i>, and <i>Persona 5</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoejimaInterview_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-SoejimaInterview-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P5details_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-P5details-104">[104]</a></sup> Soejima's drawing style is recognized as being lighter-toned than Kaneko's work on the <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OtakuKaneko_85-7"><a href="#cite_note-OtakuKaneko-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other designers have also worked on the series. For <i>Nine</i>, the developers wanted to have a new style to suit the game's original vision, so the characters were designed by animator <a href="/wiki/Yasuomi_Umetsu" title="Yasuomi Umetsu">Yasuomi Umetsu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIWnine_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIWnine-105">[105]</a></sup> Another designer for the series is Masayuki Doi, who had made a name for himself with the <i><a href="/wiki/Trauma_Center_(series)" title="Trauma Center (series)">Trauma Center</a></i> series; and designed the main characters for <i><a href="/wiki/Shin_Megami_Tensei_IV" title="Shin Megami Tensei IV">Shin Megami Tensei IV</a></i>. Inspired in his work by Kaneko's designs, he created the main characters' clothing to be a blend of Japanese and western fashions while incorporating design elements from the <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i> series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIinsight_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIinsight-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGinterview_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGinterview-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTIVstaff_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTIVstaff-108">[108]</a></sup> For the <i>Devil Survivor</i> games, Atlus were aiming to appeal to a wider audience and reinvigorate the <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise, hiring <a href="/wiki/Suzuhito_Yasuda" title="Suzuhito Yasuda">Suzuhito Yasuda</a> as character designer for this purpose. Some monsters in the second <i>Devil Survivor</i> were designed by manga artist <a href="/wiki/Mohiro_Kitoh" title="Mohiro Kitoh">Mohiro Kitoh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSartbook_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSartbook-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilDS2_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-SilDS2-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Localization">Localization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Localization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masaomark.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="240" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Masaomark.jpg/220px-Masaomark.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/86/Masaomark.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masaomark.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Masao "Mark" Inaba as seen in the Japanese versions of <i><a href="/wiki/Revelations:_Persona" title="Revelations: Persona">Persona</a></i> (left), and the <i>Revelations</i> localization (right). In the localization of the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Portable" title="PlayStation Portable">PlayStation Portable</a> version, this change was reverted.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For a long time, the <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise was not exported to western territories despite there being a recognized market. The original reasons were the heavy religious themes and symbols used, which were considered taboo in western game markets, and Nintendo's strict content guidelines for overseas releases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-6"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DengTaboo_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-DengTaboo-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1UPcontroversy_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-1UPcontroversy-112">[112]</a></sup> Later, many of these early works were prevented from coming overseas due to their age, which would have put them at a disadvantage in the modern gaming market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide2_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guide2-83">[83]</a></sup> Early entries on the PlayStation were also blocked by Sony of America's then-current approval policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upinter_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-1upinter-82">[82]</a></sup> The first title in the franchise to be localized was <i>Jack Bros.</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGhistory_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPGhistory-47">[47]</a></sup> the first role-playing game in the franchise to receive an overseas release was the first <i>Persona</i> game. This was done to give Atlus' North American branch a flagship RPG franchise that could compete with the likes of <i><a href="/wiki/Final_Fantasy" title="Final Fantasy">Final Fantasy</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Suikoden" title="Suikoden">Suikoden</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Breath_of_Fire" title="Breath of Fire">Breath of Fire</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersonaA_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersonaA-113">[113]</a></sup> According to Okada, the naming of creatures and enemies was adjusted from the main series and original Japanese release of <i>Persona</i> to make it more acceptable for an overseas audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FamOrigins_66-7"><a href="#cite_note-FamOrigins-66">[66]</a></sup> Though it managed to establish the franchise overseas, the localization was a taxing task due to a small staff and the need to change multiple aspects to suit a North American audience, including removing references to Japanese culture and changing one character from Japanese to African-American. The first <i>Persona 2</i> title, <i>Innocent Sin</i>, needed to be passed over due to shortage of manpower and the fact that development was focused on the second title, <i>Eternal Punishment</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersonaA_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersonaA-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Nocturne</i> was the first release in the <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> series to be released overseas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZinfo_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-GZinfo-114">[114]</a></sup> After the release of <i>Nocturne</i>, Atlus' overseas branches decided to add the <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> moniker to future releases within the <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise to help market the games. Despite many of the original games not bearing the moniker, it ultimately worked in Atlus' favor as, regardless of title differences, the games chosen for localization were all part of the larger <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise, and using the core <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> moniker kept all the titles under a single banner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersonaA_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersonaA-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSinterview_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSinterview-115">[115]</a></sup> Before this decision was made, the series was given the localized title <i>Revelations</i>, used for the first <i>Persona</i> and the first <i>Last Bible</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGhistory_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-RPGhistory-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSinterview_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSinterview-115">[115]</a></sup> Later, changes to titles were made to make them less unwieldy, such as with <i>Raidou Kuzunoha vs. The Soulless Army</i>. Called <i>Raidō Kuzunoha vs. The Super-Powered Army</i> in Japan, the title was altered as it sounded "goofy" in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide2_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guide2-83">[83]</a></sup> By the time <i>Strange Journey</i> was in development, the franchise had a strong presence overseas, so the team created <i>Strange Journey</i> with localization in mind: the two aspects actively linked with this were the game's setting in Antarctica as opposed to modern-day Japan, and the fact that it was not given a numeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilLocal_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-SilLocal-116">[116]</a></sup> Starting with <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV</i>, the company decided to actively promote the franchise overseas to North America, Europe and mainland Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DengTaboo_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-DengTaboo-111">[111]</a></sup> After 2016, due to Atlus USA's merger with Sega of America, <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a> took over North American publishing duties, although the Atlus brand remained intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTsega_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMTsega-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, Atlus publishes <i>Megami Tensei</i> games in Japan and North America, but as they lack a European branch, they publish titles in the region through third-party companies such as <a href="/wiki/Ghostlight" title="Ghostlight">Ghostlight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Ichi_Software" title="Nippon Ichi Software">NIS America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4Arights_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4Arights-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilGhostlight_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-SilGhostlight-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NISsurvivor_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-NISsurvivor-120">[120]</a></sup> Their latest partnership, after their deal with NIS America ended with the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Odin_Sphere" title="Odin Sphere">Odin Sphere Leifthrasir</a></i>, was with European publishing firm <a href="/wiki/Deep_Silver" title="Deep Silver">Deep Silver</a> to publish multiple titles in the region, including <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV: Apocalypse</i> and <i>Persona 5</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NISend_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-NISend-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P5euPub_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-P5euPub-122">[122]</a></sup> Atlus has occasionally published titles digitally in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PersonaPSN_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-PersonaPSN-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4Anetwork_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4Anetwork-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Prior to its popularity in the west, the game was a major franchise in Japan, having sold over 4 million units by 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZinfo_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-GZinfo-114">[114]</a></sup> By November 2014, the series has sold approximately 13 million packaged units worldwide, with the combined series grouped within the <i>Megami Tensei</i> franchise accounting for 7 million and the <i>Persona</i> series accounting for 6.2 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeriesSales_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeriesSales-125">[125]</a></sup> <i>Megami Tensei</i> is one of Atlus' best-selling and most important franchises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMTdeveloper_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-SMTdeveloper-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japanese website 4Gamer.net referred to the series as one of Japan's biggest role-playing franchises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4Ginfo_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-4Ginfo-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UGO_Networks" title="UGO Networks">UGO Networks</a> writer K. Thor Jensen cited the first <i>Megami Tensei</i> game as the first successful use of cyberpunk aesthetics in video games, saying that the series' mix of science fiction elements and the occult "create a truly unique fictional cyberpunk world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STMcyber_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-STMcyber-126">[126]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Power" title="Nintendo Power">Nintendo Power</a></i> has noted that Atlus always mixes "familiar gameplay" with surprising settings when creating games for the series, citing <i>Persona</i>, with its "modern-day horror stories" and "teams of Japanese high-school kids", as the perfect example. The editor also added that <i>Strange Journey</i> followed a similar system, calling it a "science-fiction makeover" of the series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NintendoShin_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-NintendoShin-127">[127]</a></sup> In an article about the interaction of Japanese and Western gaming culture, <a href="/wiki/1UP.com" title="1UP.com">1UP.com</a> mentioned the <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> subseries alongside <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Ichi_Software" title="Nippon Ichi Software">Nippon Ichi Software</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Disgaea" title="Disgaea"><i>Disgaea</i> series</a>. Kurt Kalata wrote: "[They] may not be big sellers, but they've garnered underground success and attracted thousands of obsessed fans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1UPtensei_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-1UPtensei-128">[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GameSpot" title="GameSpot">GameSpot</a> writer Andrew Vestal referred to the series as the third biggest series in Japan after <i>Final Fantasy</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dragon_Quest" title="Dragon Quest">Dragon Quest</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSpersona_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSpersona-129">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a>'s Matt Coleman mentioned <i>Nocturne</i> in the article "A History of Console RPGs", referring to its content as "challenging stuff for a genre that used to be all about princess saving and evil cleansing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGNhistory_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGNhistory-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Digital Devil Story: Megami Tensei II</i> and <i>Shin Megami Tensei</i> both appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Famitsu" title="Famitsu">Famitsu</a>'</i>s 2006 "Top 100 Favorite Games of All Time" audience poll at No. 58 and No. 59, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Top100-famitsu_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Top100-famitsu-131">[131]</a></sup> RPGFan's "Top 20 RPGs of the Past Decade" list was topped by the two <i>Digital Devil Saga</i> games, followed by <i>Persona 3</i> in second place, while <i>Persona 4</i> ranked fourth place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGFlist_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPGFlist-132">[132]</a></sup> Kalata, writing for <a href="/wiki/Gamasutra" title="Gamasutra">Gamasutra</a>, referred to <i>Nocturne</i> as one of the 20 essential RPGs for players of the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GamaList_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-GamaList-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GameTrailers" title="GameTrailers">GameTrailers</a> cited the Press Turn system as one of the best JRPG battle systems in existence, with particular reference to the version used in <i>Shin Megami Tensei IV</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTjrpg_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-GTjrpg-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alongside its critical acclaim, the series has garnered controversy both in Japan and overseas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1UPcontroversy_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-1UPcontroversy-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPGcontroversy_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPGcontroversy-134">[134]</a></sup> Amongst the material cited are its demon negotiation mechanic, depictions of suicide and cannibalism, <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_religion" title="Criticism of religion">religious criticism</a>, its use and mixture of Christian and occult imagery, political references, depictions of homosexuality, and its sometimes-strange demon designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1UPcontroversy_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-1UPcontroversy-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRcontroversy_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRcontroversy-135">[135]</a></sup> Specific examples have been cited by western journalists. The original release of <i>Persona</i> caused concern due to the title's religious implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIpersona_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-GIpersona-26">[26]</a></sup> 1UP.com's 2007 game awards, which ran in the March 2008 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Electronic_Gaming_Monthly" title="Electronic Gaming Monthly">Electronic Gaming Monthly</a></i>, <i>Persona 3</i> was given the "Most controversial game that created no controversy" award: the writers said "<a href="/wiki/Rockstar_Games" title="Rockstar Games">Rockstar</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hot_Coffee_mod" title="Hot Coffee mod">Hot Coffee sex scandal</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bully_(video_game)" title="Bully (video game)">Bully</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s boy-on-boy kissing's got nothing on this PS2 role-player's suicide-initiated battles or subplot involving student-teacher dating."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EdgeControversy_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-EdgeControversy-136">[136]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GamesRadar" title="GamesRadar">GamesRadar</a> included the series on its list of "Controversies Waiting to Happen", saying that the lack of public outcry was due to its niche status when compared to other series with similar content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRcontroversy_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRcontroversy-135">[135]</a></sup> Writing for 1UP.com in a later article, Kalata traced this use of controversial content back to the <i>Digital Devil Story</i> novels, which depicted violence and rape committed by demons, and said that "Such violence is not particularly rare in the land of Japanese animation, but it became even more disturbing in [<i>Megami Tensei II</i>]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1UPcontroversy_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-1UPcontroversy-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Megami_Tensei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-DDS1release-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-DDS1release_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-DDS1release_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.rpgamer.com/games/megten/megten1/megten1.html" rel="nofollow">"Digital Devil Monogatari: Megami Tensei"</a>.
